L1263319	H	41.0526638	-124.2568016	2012-09-08	08:45:00	We went out to Trinidad Canyon about 22 miles offshore. Overall bird abundance was really low and the trip was somewhat slow but we did have a couple of highlights like a Tufted Puffin and a Laysan Albatross. We also had very nice experiences with 5-6 Humpback Whales just beyond 5-6 miles west of Trinidad. URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS103819930	1709	species	Laysan Albatross	Phoebastria immutabilis			1			United States	US	California	US-CA	Mendocino	US-CA-045		Arena Cove	L333704	H	38.914641	-123.708449	2010-12-23	13:55:00			obs22550	Chuck 	Vaughn	S7301383	eBird - Stationary Count	EBIRD	5			1	1		1	0		
URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS107918151	1709	species	Laysan Albatross	Phoebastria immutabilis			1		Unknown Sex, Adult (1)	United States	US	California	US-CA	Mendocino	US-CA-045		Arena Cove and fishing pier	L1088572	P	38.9143942	-123.711226	2011-01-05	15:45:00	Louis Bevier and I next visited Arena Cove in the hope of seeing "Al" the resident Laysan Albatross that has been seen here during winter for the last 17 years.  After missing this bird twice in the past, I finally managed to see it today.  Upon our arrival, we parked and walked out the fishing pier to find the albatross loafing on the water a short distance offshore.  Our visit was relatively brief and I spent most of my time concentrating on the albatross, so we did not see many species here; however, given the near lack of vegetation around the pier and the small size of the cove, there were probably not all that many birds present in the first place.  It was clear, with a trace (<5 knot) breeze from the west, and temperatures in the low 50s.  3:45-4:15 pm.	Almost immediately upon reaching the end of the pier, I spotted this bird as it swam 100-150 meters offshore and slightly to the north of our position.  The albatross was therefore moderately distant and it was distinctly side-lit, so my views of this bird were not quite what I had hoped for.  Typically this bird swam around slowly and picked at the kelp stalks, at times ripping the leaves off and tossing them aside before nibbling at the end of each stalk.  This bird also swam up to each surfer that passed by, and I saw two surfers reach out and touch it.  The albatross preened briefly and I once saw it flap its wings, but we never saw it in flight.--This was a large, gull-like bird with a particularly long bill that had a sharply hooked tip.  I estimated that the bill was one-and-a-quarter times as long as the head was wide.  It tapered evenly from a heavy base along a culmen that was slightly concave before curving downward sharply at the tip.  The forehead was weakly sloping and the crown had a somewhat flattened appearance, though the head as a whole was rounded.  The large head sat atop a relatively long, slim neck.  The body was heavy and the folded wings created a bit of bulk.  Despite the great length of the spread wings, the folded wings reached only to the tip of the tail, and the primary projection seemed to be relatively short.  The tail was seemingly rounded and conspicuously short, barely reaching beyond the tips of the undertail coverts.  The legs were placed rather far back on the body, and they seemed to be moderately stout, but I never saw the feet.  I also saw little on the spread wings when the bird flapped, though I did see that they were conspicuously long and slim.--This bird had striking plumage patterns that combined white on the head, neck, breast, flanks, and undertail coverts, with a sooty brown color on the back, scapulars, and wings.  The rump and uppertail coverts, seen only briefly, seemed to be white, but the tail was dark.  In all, I detected little contrast amid the dark regions of the back and wings.  Rather conspicuous, however, was the dusky "mascara" on the face that extended back from the base of the upper mandible to the upper edge of the eye, and then a short way back along the upper edge of the auriculars.  Overall, the auriculars and the front part of the face had a grayish suffusion that seemed to blend into the black lores.  The demarcation between the white cap and the grayish face was a sharp one, but the rear and lower edges of the gray face blended into the white of the sides of the neck.  I never did see the color of the throat.--The bill was mostly pink, but it blended to medium-gray at the tip.  The eyes were dark, and I had at least some impression of a white eyelid, though I cannot say that I saw this at all well.  The legs were pale grayish.	obs54638	Curtis	Marantz	S7583805	eBird - Stationary Count	EBIRD	30	.1		2	1	G229017	1	0

Sequoia Audubon Society ran their first ever pelagic out of Half Moon Bay, San Mateo, today and it was fantastic! This tops the one last week for the San Francisco Bay Bird Observatory where three species of albatross were seen (BF, Laysan, and Short-tailed). Today we struck gold with a White-chinned Petrel, if accepted a new record for California! The bird first was in the wake of the boat and wheeled off, fortunately we were able to make it come in to some chum and it gave some close passes before sitting behind the boat. Everyone on the boat had looks and we were able to study the features of the species. The bulky structure, short tail, large body,big belly of the bird were noticeably different from a Flesh-footed Shearwater (which we saw soon after this sighting). As well the bill was dull yellow to the tip, with each bill plate nicely outlined in black.--Zooming up on photos we could see the small white chin patch, but this was not visible in the field. The bird was fresh looking and even in wear state. I should also mention we did see one or perhaps two Laysan Albatross today as well. --Others on board; Jim Lomax, JOhn Sterling, Jennifer Rycenga, Denise Wight, Kris Olson. Many photos taken. Conception	L1828856	P	34.4523206	-122.1995544	1993-02-06	06:30:00	a 28 hour pelagic from Ventura harbor, aboard "Vanguard," to reach deep water at 34.45N,122.05W, about 75 nmi W of Pt. Conception, SBA, and return. This meant nearly 12 hours of light over a 120 mi transect, all in search of Parakeet Auklets, found in this area 2 weeks ago by P. Pyle on research cruise. This area is deep water (2000 fathoms deep). Birders aboard included Rita Carratello, Guy McCaskie, Phil Rostron, Marc Fenner, Brian Daniels, Bruce Broadbooks, Mitch Heindel, Monte Taylor, Sherman Suter (who provided lat-longs to me later), et al. We all had bunks for the night out and night back (left 10 p.m. on 5 Feb, return 2 a.m. on 7 Feb). Weather was incredible good, seas mostly flat Beaufort 2, and only Beaufort 3 in afternoon. 5' swell, sunny to partly cloudy. Mammals also good, including 6 Baird's Beaked Whale, 20 Short-finned Pilot Whale, 15 Killer Whale, 200 Grampus, 150 N. Rightwhale Dolphin, 5 N. Fur Sea, 1 N. Elephant Seal, 1 Calif. Sea-lion. Arguelo	L1829369	P	34.583	-121.883	1993-02-15	10:00:00	a pelagic trip from Port San Luis, aboard "Eclipse," in an effort to reach deep water where Parakeet Auklets had been found a month before, and glimpsed on 6 Feb on a trip from Ventura (q.v.), but the boat was too slow (9 knots top speed) was we never reached the right area during the 15 hour trip. The trip was thus mis-advertised and considered a bust for those aboard, including me, Rita Carratello, Guy McCaskie, Bruce Broadbooks, Bill Principe, Hank & Priscilla Brodkin, Brad Schram, Jim Snyder, Allen Royer, Monte Taylor, Shawneen Finnegan, Kimball Garrett, Sherman Suter. The route was WSW from Port San Luis, so first 4 hours out and last 4 hours on return were in SLO waters (see separate checklist); we reached the point I have marked for this checklist (34.583N,121.883W) at 2 pm, in deep water (2000 fathoms) but had limited time here and soon started the return leg. Weather decent early (sunny with some clouds) in Beaufort 3 conditions, but got rough (Beaufort 5) by afternoon, and clouded over with threat of rain. I did not diligently indicate the county in my notes for the day, except the scarcer species, but allocate the common ones between SLO/SBA by time rule. We did not have anything unusual, and only limited mammals (1 Humpback, 30 Pac WS Dolphin, 1 N Elephant Seal, 2 Cal Sea-lion). URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS101176723	1709	species	Laysan Albatross	Phoebastria immutabilis			X			United States	US	Hawaii	US-HI	Kauai	US-HI-007	US-HI_3251	Kilauea Point NWR	L266182	H	22.2320374	-159.4022757	2003-05-30	08:30:00	My next stop was at Kilauea Point NWR. I got there 30 minutes before it opens, but there is an overlook that you can see the entire area from. From this vantage, before the refuge opened I had an adult LAYSAN ALBATROSS* sail over, and RED-FOOTED BOOBIES resting less than 75 feet away. RED-TAILED TROPICBIRDS* were numerous, and I got a brief look at a WHITE-TAILED TROPICBIRD* as it flew into its nest and out of sight. GREAT FRIGATEBIRDS were soaring over the point as well, occasionally harassing a BOOBY, but mostly just making flying look way too easy.  Finally the refuge opened, and I made my way in. Immediately I was face to face with two Hawaiian Geese feeding in the refuge managers yard! Views were great at all the seabirds, including looks at LAYSAN chicks resting on a distant knoll. After following the moans, I found a WEDGE-TAILED SHEARWATER outside its burrow. This was only a couple of feet off of the walkway, under the dense shrubs. The numbers of RED-FOOTED BOOBIES nesting here blew my away. The stack off of the end of the point is supposed to be a regular place for MASKED BOOBY, but this species eluded me all trip. I highly recommend this spot to anyone, birders or not. ----	adult sailed over while I was waiting for gate to open. Bird hoovered mostly over ocean surface between waves; unfortunately, the 4-5 waves meant for a quick ID.  Overall bird was dark brown with rounded head, squared off tail,  bill was dark including top salt extruder,  dark feet hanging down, wings flapped about 2 times- slightly up and bent at carpel.  Wings: had bicolor effect of dark brown with light brown. Rump & Flanks:  we could tell there was whitish color on flanks that seemed to go up abit but the white caps interfered with  discerning a band even though sun was behind us. URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS124139209	1709	species	Laysan Albatross	Phoebastria immutabilis			1			United States	US	Washington	US-WA	Clallam	US-WA-009		Olympic NP--Rialto Beach	L864689	H	47.9196209	-124.6385193	1995-10-15		With Bob Plehal on sight-seeing vacation to Victoria British Columbia and Washington (Thurs.-Monday, Oct. 12-16); ferried from Victoria to Port Angeles and drove across the Olympic Peninsula to La Push and Rialto Beach before continuing to Olympia to spend the night and visiting Mt. Ranier the following day; cloudy, windy and cold at the beach around mid-day (a light drizzle may have been falling part of the time); albatross came within about 100-200 yards from shore (distance estimated from memory as I enter this on August 12, 2011) and was easily visible with 7X binoculars; sighting was on the 14th or the 15th (15th more likely).	large gull-like seabird immediately recognized as an albatross: larger than any gull and with narrow, much longer wings held stiffly outstreched as it soared effortlessly low over the water without any flapping; at the time, I did not know species names and did not realize until I returned home from my trip that several species were possible - Black-footed is much more likely seen from shore, but I remembered this bird as "gull-like", with a very dark mantle (upper wings and back), white head and body, large light-colored bill, and dark eye patch - like "typical" albatrosses I had seen on nature shows, the only other species ever recorded in the area are the extremely rare Short-tailed Albatross, which has a white back, and the Shy/White-capped Albatross, which looks larger and bulkier with less contrast between head and back than what I thought I remembered; I have had this bird on my life list as a probable Laysan Albatross ever since.	obs103163	Drew	Dickert	S8665934	eBird - Casual Observation	EBIRD				2	0		1	1
